Sorry for the late drywall hanging tip, but never have a drywall seam run above or below the corner of a window or doorway as you did at 13:30 when you hung your first sheet.  Expansion and contraction of the house will cause the mud to crack at that seam.  By now, you 've probably already developed a crack along the seam of that doorway.   Your first sheet should have started at the wall and covered the doorway.   Once you get the top sheet hung, use your reciprocating saw to cut out the doorway.  When hanging drywall, you have to plan for doors and windows so the sheet does not terminate at the edge of a door or window.

Drywall is so easy. I don't know, why so many people were telling you not to do it.  It gets easier when you cut on the white side and break it. Then, you just have to cut the brown paper side of the plasterboard and job done. When I fix plasterboards to the studs I mark the centre of the stud (on the floor and ceiling) and use chalk line to mark where the screws have to go.  For fine tuning use a block of wood. You can "shave" the plasterboard this way. It's messy, but for small adjustments it's much better than cutting it with a knife.

Eamon, didn't anyone teach you how to negotiate the trailer alone?  The following is how I used to do my boat trailer by myself. 1. Find or cut yourself a stump 8"-10" diameter by about 2" taller than the height of the hitch on your truck. 2. Place the stump next to and close to the end of the trailer tongue 3. Lift the tongue of the trailer onto the stump,  4. Back the truck up just under the tongue,  5, Remove the stump, lowering the trailer tongue onto the hitch ball 6. Make necessary connections and drive away. ONWARRRRD!

Yes you can.  I did it.  If I can do it, anyone can.
Secret to drywall...make seams as few and no gaps...then tape with a good tape (paper vs other kinds) use THIN coats of mud, let dry, take wet sponge and go over, take off any excess, let dry, repeat process until you have an almost perfectly blended seam.  Let dry...sand.  Brush sanding remains off, base coat, then top coat of paint.  Ceilings are especially difficult...but you can rent a thingie which will hold dry wall panel up to ceiling so that you can attach it.  If I were you, I would pay someone to do ceilings...someone who is experienced!  Good luck.  Love your videos.  You two are so adorable and fun to watch together.  Blessings!

How do you remove snow from a roof with a rope? First, you should tie your hammer on to one end of the rope, creating a makeshift weight. Once you're on the roof, toss the hammer on to the ground (but don't forget to hold on to the other side of the rope!) Once you've dropped the hammer, wrap the rope around the edge of the piled snow.

I am currently drywalling â€œby my selfâ€, a bathroom and bedroom. Ceiling and walls, everything. You canâ€™t have too much plaster. Fill in those gaps first with plaster, then lay tape on wet plaster and use more plaster to fill in the wholes in and around the tape. Then use even more plaster to even it out. You would rather have a little too much plaster then not enough! Really. Itâ€™s a pain in the $&&, to sand it even and level, but that to me is the hardest part; sanding. Good luck. And remember to use many layers of paint (white ) first. Then paint your others colors if you want.  Oh by the way: always put factory edge cuts to factory edge cuts. Put your cut edges in corners and on the floors. This always makes it easier to cover your mistakes when cutting drywall. Measure twice cut once, but I find with drywall measure four times and cut once. Measure and measure until your tape measurer breaks! LoL â€œjust kiddingâ€œ.

We did our own drywall with no experience and having only watched one tutorial. It's not nearly as difficult as people make it seem-though the mudding takes a lot of practice to get seamless it was more important to us to learn how to do it ourselves than it was to have it look perfect and it will save you loads of money. My advice is start slow and build and taper every mud layer-better to need to add another coat then to have too much even though you'll sand it down later. As you're mudding mix the solution with water in little bits at a time since it starts drying as soon as you mix it! You guys can do it!

Hanging drywall properly is more important than taping properly.  All of your doors and windows are going to crack and all of the vertical seams that run from floor to ceiling are going to crack. If you want it to still look nice in two years,  redo it all now..... 1. Always hang sheets horizontally,  even short pieces.  2. Always hang a full sheet across the top of a door or window,  then cut it out. 3. Leave a half inch gap at the floor.  4. The smaller the gaps,  the easier it'll be to mud 5. That six inch overhang is no good..... watch videos by "Vancouver carpenter" to learn how to mud.  That guy is a PRO.... You don't know what you're doing,  but neither did I two years ago.  Google before you start a project and you'll be soooo much further ahead..... I agree with your decision still though. Better to try and mess it up than to pay someone else and learn nothing.
Always remember "Measure Twice Cut One" and don't freakout about the mudding and taping.
Do not do the mud and tape, please!

I just finished hanging and taping drywall in  an entire house. I watched  2 Canadian you tubers and had no issues. Vancouver carpenter and home renovision. Highly recommend them. FYI, I turn 60 this year. If I can do it you can. Taping is easy if you follow them, I only sand on the last coat to minimize dust. I did buy a chesp drywall lift, highly recommended for ceiling. Get the bags of 90 minute mix for the first coats. Only mix half a bag. Don't try to be perfect on first coats. Don't sand till end, just knock off high spots with drywall blade. Soak your tape in water to prevent bubbles. Final coat use pre mix and a keep a spray bottle of water handy  in case it dries out on you.
Insulate now will be glad you did in the future, also should not be breaking your sheet rock (dry wall) on the same joint will make finishing tougher. God Bless
You will find that it is best to put up the largest size piece of drywall you can. Try to have only one horizontal mud line and NO verticlel lines except corners . Every INCH of less mudding will save so much time     CHEERS    Steve h.

When we started this "adventure" the bathroom didn't have insulation or drywall up. What we had was grocery store plastic bags stuffed here and there as some kind of insulation. The secret to mud & tape... wet the tape, use 5" knife for first coat of mud.... don't worry about gaps/divots/holes, they'll fill in later... use 8" knife blade for coats 2 & 3/skim coat... smooth as you go... wait for it to dry a little then knock down any ridges to save on sanding.

I was 26 when we bought our first house. We tackle just about everything ourselves. Drywall and mudding is about patience and learning when you should stop! A few tips: 1. Install drywall vertical, not horizontal. There are special divets on the long sides of the drywall sheets to help you mud. If you install vertical then you should only have two drywall sheets touching for the most part. If you install horizontal then you can have 4 sheets touching and those divets end up running along the ceiling or along the floor! 2. I prefer the netted drywall tape because it's sticky and someone can tape while another starts mudding. 3. When it comes to mudding, less is best. I still need to remind myself of this after 7 years! GOODLUCK! Can't wait to see your progress next week â¤â¤â¤
No sugar coating, but what you need to read.....A few things amateurs are clueless about..... NEVER have a 4 way intersection. T intersections are better. Especially when taping and spackling (USA language). Stagger the boards so no 4 way seams happen.  (Saw you did 4 ways every opportunity). Always try to lay the 4x8 sheets across the studs, and the more studs, the more stable. Put the sheet against the ceiling first and press it up so it touches the ceiling. Level is the only thing you can trust. Use it. DON"T screw the screw heads so deep they rip the paper. This eventually will let the sheetrock pull from the studs from daily vibrations over the years.. Dimpling the screws and/or nail heads gives the spackle more surface area to adhere to and remain stable longer. If you have never spackled from start to finish, you will be sorry after you paint and see how bad you did. Getting it done properly is an art that comes from experience. Floating and working with different sized knives to perfection doesn't happen for a long time. Nor does polishing! Do it right.... Pay the price. Or the price you wind up paying will be painful. Sometimes Luck comes from educated decisions, rather than over confidence that hasn't been earned. Hope this makes sense and helps! And pad out headers that sit too low rather than cutting the studs around them. DOH.

hints to mud and tape; cut away any raised flags of paper from the sheets, don't use fiberglass tape for the corners use paper, do buy the corner trowel for the inside corners 2", 6" 12" knives for the rest. Use corner mold for the outside corners, don't worry about corners and edges that are covered with trim. mudding is not about flat it about smooth (within reason) seams take at least 3 coats 1- fill crack and stick tape on smoothly 3" wide +/-(tape doesn't need to be a continuous piece one end to the other) 2- cover tape 4"-6" wide, 3- smooth skim coat 10"-14" wide, repeat #3 if required. Sand only enough to take the edges off the previous coats, successive coats cover the edges and fill the holes, the smoother you get with the knife the less sanding you need to do but don't over work the mud. And clean up tools regularly don't return mud to the pail small bits of dried mud will make smooth mudding imposable when you see lines in your smoothing stroke stop an clear the knife chunks in the mud will make the edge of your knife work like a rake. Clean up sanding dust right away wipe it from the wall as well it will mix with new mud and make chunks ^ and will spread and dirty everything it is much like mood dust of the desert it will get everywhere. Wear a N95 mask for sanding and clean up. Vacuum only with a hepa filter it will just blow the finest particles everywhere if you don't.  It takes patience and perseverance this is 80% skill 15% art, 2% black magic and 3% divine intervention. Good luck it mostly takes practice.
You're flat out crazy but it'll be a learning experience!
In UK we tape and mud, then skim the whole sheet so thereâ€™s no sanding. Once dry you apply and ova glue and water mix to seal the finishing plaster.

DIY - DW tips: Might be too late.... Longest sheets possible, fewest seems, leave a small gap (1/8" ish) at all joints for compound to go in (not enough compound under the tape, guaranteed crack). Minimize butt joints, use paper tape not fibre, set the tape with sheetrock 90. Use Durabond 90 (not sandable but solid as rock) to adhere the overhanging DW to the corner at the boxed in duct, and other tricky spots. Boxed in duct will put lots of stress on joints from expansion movement. Use regular DW compound for finish coats, it's easier to sand and makes a nice finish for priming and paint.

If you do more drywalling, overlap your pieces to help prevent cracking. Over windows and doorways, don't just cut a piece for the top, use a bigger piece and cut out the window/door so you have continuous drywall around the opening. Hard to screw up mudding, you will just mud, sand, sand, sand, sand. Use less mud and apply more coats if you need to - sanding is for losers! Love your content!
Mud and tape: give it time. I usually do three coats. First with a 4 inch knife, filling the gaps and covering the yellow tape. 2nd coat with a 10 inch knife smoothing the edges of the first coat. 3rd coat with a 12 inch knife to finish it all. The idea is to make sure the mud is as smooth as possible because the real pain is not mudding it's sanding. So the less sanding you do the easier it is. For the corners I recommend investing in a reinforced tape called "sure corner". It will save time and booboos.

You need to separate the vertical joints on your walls, each vertical joint (butt joint) should only be 4â€™ long on your 8â€™ high walls. Your splitting the top sheet and bottom sheet in the middle of the same stud. That makes for an 8â€™ butt joint from ceiling to floor, which when taped and mudded will make for a very wide finished joint from ceiling to floor. Basically you will have a large vertical hump in the middle of all your walls from ceiling to floor where each of these butt joints line up unless you can find an experienced drywall finisher! A couple drywall terms: A flat joint is the seem that runs through the middle of your wall from corner to corner and has the factory tapered edge. A butt joint is the seem that runs vertical, where two drywall sheets match up in the middle of a stud.

Don't know how far behind your videos are, but if you haven't started tape and mud yet, be sure to check out Home Renovision DIY's "How to Drywall" playlist. He has great tips and makes it look easy (it isn't.) Love your videos!
I LOVE to mud. It is really satisfying. Just get yourselves some basic tools - a wide 6 in FLEXIBLE putty knife, a palate or handheld "hod" to hold joint compound, have a few smaller putty knives for small areas and maybe a triangle for those spots where nothing else will fit AND a LARGER 12-14 in knife to fair in the edges after the first couple of coats. Start with the 6 in knife and after a couple of coats of mud use the larger to fair the mud feather thin at the edges. I use only the mesh fiberglass tape for seams and corners and I'd recommend that too. Paper seems tricker to get to stick for we part-time tapers and the mesh is strong.   One of the most important tricks I learned from watching professional drywallers is to use your 6 in knife to knock off your high mud spots once it drys between coats. It makes far less a mess than sanding and if you are careful and use large knives that fair your edges well it makes for minimal sanding at the end. Remember, you can fill in concavities or voids as you go but anything above the surface has to be knocked off or sanded. Also, do one side of the corner the first day - that way you can leave the little "bead' that rolls over and that will chip right off the next day. And then do the second edge of the corner. Also making your first cut on the white side where the paper is much thinner and then snap. Mesh tape is much easier for the beginner. If you use paper tape, put mud on wall then put on paper tape and press into mud. Wait until this dries before putting another coat of mud on top. If you get in a hurry and put another coat on too soon the paper tape will pull away and create a bubble in your seam. Mesh tape can be put right on the drywall first before any mud. Good luck

I'm an old drywaller/contractor who hung and taped drywall for almost 20 years and glad to give you a few tips. One tip, when you hanging drywall you need to stagger your butt joints, meaning don't put the top and bottom joints on the same stud but stagger them. Also if you can, run your drywall over the door header past the door jamb at least 6 or 8 inches. Both of these will help prevent future cracking of your joints. Especially the door jamb given the stress the jamb will create over the door.

Eamon, take it from someone who has been dealing with back problems for nearly 50 years. I either sit up straight in a chair, or lay on my stomach on the floor, or I have one of those workman's back braces I'll slip the ice pack or heated rice pack into so I can walk around. Put an ice pack on it first, not too cold, use a kitchen towel or paper towels to insulate a bit. No more than 10-15 minutes of that. Then MOIST heat for another 10-15 minutes. Those packs filled with raw rice that you stick in the microwave are good to use. Again a kitchen towel can avoid the rice from burning your skin. Avoid twisting motions or lifting anything above 10 lbs for a few days. Tylenol or Motrin or Aleve. Mudding tips... use sticky tape to tape seams, get some nice blades (especially a wide one) and a 90 degree blade.  First coat use heavy mud then others may switch to lightweight.  Many small coats better than putting on too thick.  Get big sponge to smooth out after dry (No sandpaper= no dust).  Everyone told us not to do it but it was easy.  But if you do want to make a mess with dust, we are sure everyone will enjoy the footage...
when you do the mud and taping do very light coats and let them dry between coats. And when you sand the walls make sure you change your air filter in your central gas unit often so you don't fill your duct work full of dust.

******** READ ME ******  Make sure wherever there will be lights casting shadows, the joints are super smooth.  You'll really notice them when it's done and painted.  That is critical... First day on your channel - love it! (found via comment on Trent and Allie's channel)  Takes me back to my early '30's when I bought a "project" on Deer Isle in coastal Maine.  Small owner-built house (think he procured materials at the dump and in other folks barn lofts - rural dumps were actually pretty great back then) - rough-cut lumber, nothing square or plumb - crappy rock wool insulation (sinuses still remember that sans-mask removal) , cheap panelling, ancient linoleum, and of course rotting joists, single-pain windows, and inoperative septic system (discovered post-sale):  a 50 gal. rusted out barrel (still gunning for the "inspector" I paid).  Positives:  great oak recycled staircase and red oak flooring, good house proportions, a really old "marginal" barn (1860's), southern exposure,  and "down the hill" to the ocean.  I was single, hated my job (really just my boss), and wanted to live alone.  Working full-time, a 2hr. one-way commute away, and NO money.  This was the mid-80's, so there was still affordable real wood available for flooring, trim, doors, windows, wonderful used brick for the chimney re-build, leftover granite at the abandoned quarries for steps and garden walls, used, great condition Vermont Casting wood stove, and folks who needed work at a reasonable rate who were desperate enough to let an inexperienced woman work with them.  Can TOTALLY relate to hanging sheetrock when no square/plumb and "sticking-out bits".  No screws for me either - all done with a hammer and sheetrock nails like the ones you pulled.  I actually liked the taping and mudding - sort of an artistic endeavor, but took forever.  Laid ceramic tiles in my kitchen - another project where I learned a lot, so of course saw all my flaws.  Installed "real" multi-piece built-up baseboards and crown moulding - only seen in mansions these days.  Also made the mistake of putting cedar siding on the house and using galvanized nails which wind up leaving gray streaks, so had to set and fill every nail hole before painting.  I loved doing the whole thing (took several years by the way), learning so much, but don't think I could do it again at my age.  So I will watch you guys and gals with all the new materials, tools and accessories and enjoy vicariously.

Taping the joints and corners is not too difficult. The key is taking the time to do 3 coats with good sanding between coats. Resist the urge to go thick and try to sand it smooth. Put 3 thin coats and sand each smooth. Itâ€™s very dusty and hard work but it can be done well if you take your time. Get the right tools also. The final coat needs to be installed with a 12 inch trowel to assure a smooth transition at the edge of the mud.

Iâ€™ve done DIY drywall lots of times. Last time was when I remodeled my bathroom and replaced all the plumbing. (I was 69! And I am a Great Grandma). There are cheap drywall screw bits at your hardware store.  They keep u from putting the screws in too far and breaking the drywall. I like the self sticking yellow drywall tape. Then u can tape it all. Then mud. And you donâ€™t have to do both in the same step.  You need to bed your tape w a thick layer of mud. I use a 4â€ rounded edge drywall trowel; to put first layer on and then a 6â€ to push it in and remove.  Flare it out at edges.  The next day; Sand or use a damp lightly any ridges. And to flare any thick edges. Next layer I put on mud w 6â€ and wipe off w 8â€ trowel. Next day sand lightly any too thick and ridges. Put on skim coat w 6â€ wipe off w 12â€. Sand smooth BE CAREFUL never sand into the surrounding paper or youâ€™ll have to sand your primer paint! To knock the ruff off.  Suggest you do spare room first and get your technique down. Then other rooms. Living room last as that is the room everyone will see!

You can easily mud and tape, just be ready for a lot of really fine dust.   Move everything out of the room before sanding.  It gets everywhere.  Wear a dust filter mask.   You can also have someone hold a wet/dry vac under the sanding block to help collect the dust.   Don't get discouraged after the first coat; each subsequent application gets better.   It can take awhile to dry if it's cold (which appears to be the case at your cabin), so count on some time between each coat.  My husband and I have drywalled several rooms and done lots of patching.   It's actually kind of fun.

my advice on mud and tap.. invest  in a quality pan and knife.. the goal is to make a smooth seamless transition from one sheet to the next.. its not easy and might need to be fixed several times.. so the cheaters way is to texture the entire wall.. a pan and knife texture is simple and only requires inconsistent strokes. the goal is to hide the tape and cover the screws.. after it has dried you sand the rough surfaces and ridges, then paint.. just a warning.. closets are a great place to start until you get the hang on it.. if you don't have a closet practice with a spare sheet of drywall or plywood till you have a method you like..
Stagger the butt joints (4' end of sheet) so you don't have a seam that is directly above the other to eliminate the possibility of cracking and having a harder time finishing the joint.  The other way to make the butt joints easier to finish is to buy drywall shims (thin cardboard strips) that you put on each stud the other of where the butt joint will be.  This will give you a little bit of a dish at the butt joint so it is easier to tape and finish.

Funny story... I left this comment first on Nomadic Movement channel ...  it was for you...    The main goal with mud to to try to avoid sanding,  use thin coats .  Avoid ridges and bumps ,  if you apply it neatly you avoid the worst part which is sanding.  Use different size taping knife for each coat.  Working from 6,8 12â€ knifes for each coat,  plan on the mud being about 12â€ wide for each seam.   Alway pull your knife along the seam.. not across,  using long strokes in the directional the seam...  it is temping to try short strokes across the seams but is is much harder to control that way.... 3 or 4 thin coats are much easier that a lot of sanding.

Eamon, I hung rock in CA after high school. Hanging the board is easy. Measure, mark your line, score with a drywall knife, bend, score paper on bend side and snap back. Why would anyone say that plasterboarding is 'too hard'? It's not rocket-science. I've never seen the point of plastering after you've put the boards up. Just tape the joints and smooth them then paint it all. Jointing compound is a hell of a lot easier to use than real plaster because you can sand it easily. Well worth 20 quid a bag rather than 6 for the versatility. Any fool can get that right. Takes a while, but so does everything. If you do decide to try and plaster it you'll find that actually plastering large areas and getting it flat needs real practice. It's about the only DIY thing I've found hard. I still DIY but your first few goes will be pretty crappy and you'll have to do a lot of filling. Also _never_ try to use plaster that is significantly out of date (a few months is OK, but not years). It really does go off way too fast to be able to get it smooth and flat.  I would have put so much more insulation in that place! You just seem to have 3 or 4 inches(~100mm) of foam in the wall. Nice and airtight, but it's proper cold where you are: twice that much would be appropriate. Glad to see some attention to airtightness though. Overall it's looking pretty good.

hey guys, if you donÂ´t mind some advice. About dry lining, you should do over lapping, it will prevent cracks in the future. Next to door jam, donÂ´t place plasterboard just next to it all the way up strait, but cut over the door, at least to the middle, that will also prevent cracks coused when you shut the door and the stud on the lock side will shake. When boxing in ducts or pipes, in the outside corner use timber all the way from bottom up, it will be sturdier and better, again, to prevent cracks. Type and joint (as it is called in the UK) I personally would hire somebody to do it for me, even thou I have experience with it, but if I would want to do my house and want to have nice clean lines, would most probably hire pro. The think is, you might think that it looks nice, when you did it and send it, but once you paint it and light will shine on it, you will see every single uneven bit made either by sand paper, or trowel. And repair it after it is painted is gonna by nightmare. Never the less, if you decide to do it, I recommend to buy premixed mud, it might be a bit more expensive, but you will use every bit of it, meanwhile powder one, you wont use everything as when it starts to go off, it is hard to work with and you end up throwing out quite a bit of it. Not talking about the time you loos mixing it and cleaning bucket after every mix, as if you leave it with dry bits, they will fall in the new mix and will make marks as you trowel it on the wall.  Sooo, donÂ´t want to rain on your parade, I just think that this might help you to get better. Good luck :)

when you mud the first coat..taping..if you have big gaps, then add some plaster OF PARIS OR DUROC to the joint compound..it dries quickly so do, small batches..it can enable you to fill gaps bigger than half an inch and tape it in there..always, take off as much plaster as you can initially..unless you have a big valley, then you can start building up around the joint 2 feet wide with very large trowels...big trowels of 15 inches wide are the secret..initially, the taping is done with 3-4 inch wide trowels..but building up the joint well takes large trowels..The Pros hardly sand in the end because they scrape most of the excess off, then fill in the small knicks later..3 coats..know when to stop on each..>Have lots of Heat for Drying..some even install Fans.
Wear masks, and keep the door closed in the bedroom, or plastic off the living room area as you will get plaster dust EVERYWHERE when sanding. Easy - but laborious. Youâ€™ve got this!!

Eamon i love your smooth move when you turned around and snaped the drywall in half. With your hat on you looked like the guy on the Indy project. Another You Tuber. You are doing a great job on the dry wall work. When you do the taping use the netting typ of tape it has a sticky back, it's easer to use than the paper tape. When doing the corners, fold the tape in half the length of the wall ,and push it in the corners.Use a 6 inch wide trowell, using drywall compound apply a light coat to the area's and let dry for a day.Do not use rapit set compound for it drys too fast to work with it.Then on the second and final coat, take your trowell and just go over the dryed compound just to knock of the high points. Then apply the second coat of compond with a 12 inch trowell and cover a wider area than the tape.Its called floating the wall.Once it is dry you can lightly sand and your done.When doing the corners with compond take your finger and run it down the wall and it will give you a great finish.Use metal corner bead where two drywall boards meet, also after nailing them on tape and mud. Also use a compound mud tray.

put mud on in layers. Use three different width knives.  A narrow one for the first layer to fill the cracks between sheets, installing tape and covering screws.  Use a wider one to cover all the cracks in the first layer.  Use a foot wide one for a final layer which makes a nice smooth finish.

Taping and mudding drywall is not that hard to do. It's just time consuming. First you run your mud down your joints and then you lay your tape down your joints and then you mud back over it let it sit and dry overnight then give it a very light sanding and then you get a wide spreader and then you spread more mud down the joints let it stand and dry for 24 hours then give it a light sanding again and then you repeat the process for the third time and then you want to make sure that you send everything flush on the third time and all your screws you do the same thing you just mud over it the screw dimples let it dry you were like sanding mud over them again give it another light sanding after 24 hours do it again for the third time process is all done like I said it's time consuming and it's dusty from the sanding. You want to make sure to use drywall sending screen is designed for sending drywall.

Looking good!  Definitely concur with many other comments about mudding: thin layers, dry/sand between.  Also, if you have any wide seams, put some mud in them, let it dry and sand level before applying the seam tape.  This keeps the tape from pushing into the seam and gives a smoother look when finished.

Me and my husband renovated a tiny house/ workshop few tips and drywall the first time like we did !!  - fiberglass tape is easier to work with if you are new to taping - quality small and big (1ft)  drywall spreading knives and an angled mud knife for corners are essential  - use a flat trowel tray to scoop mud onto the wall  instead of a bucket, it is easier to hold and work with at a moderate pace and easier control and how much mud you put on your trowel. Like a paint pallate.  if you do use a bucket get a metal one, not a plastic one - wetter mud is easier to work with ( not to wet or it will drip, you are looking for between play dough and thick soup ) but some cracking can happen, you can fill in cracks for the  second layer.  -  you can create a stronger joint if there is a small 1/8th of an inch between panels because it gives the mud something to grab in to. Works great when you dry wall a ceiling as well.  - we highly encourage proper PPE when sanding the mud when its dry, open a window, use a respirator, the dust gets everywhere!! Not good to breathe in or get in your eyes.  - if you have any large gaps between panels do a layer of mud first to fill it in, smooth out, tape and then do your second layer, sand, and then a finishing layer.  - drywall over screws! Its easy to forget. - try to keep the mud clean, try to make sure there's no dry specks in the mud or it will come out bumpy.  - if you have something bowed out and crooked like a door frame you can use the mud to build out the wall/ door frame to make it level, and smoothed out with panel edges to make it a clean look.  - painters tape off any edges  you don't want mud on like a window, if you do happen to get in on the window you can clean it off with hot water. Putting tape down first can help save time cleaning!  - mud takes multiple sessions, layers and takes time, don't do it all at once. Leave time for every layer to dry! You will be fighting cracks the whole time and will come out looking sloppy.  - Take breaks during drying to avoid pain for arms, hands, wrists etc.  - other than all of that ! Watch alot of professional videos on YouTube for more tricks and tips, it saved our butts when my husband spent hours watching YouTube(:  I hope this helps guys!!! You're awesome and drywall is possible! Doing it yourself save so much money instead of hiring someone to do it. I had to help and share cause we totally remember the struggle! Let me know if you have any other questions that I could help you answer! (:

I'm concerned about the old dry wall. When we remodeled an old home, we learned that we should have used primers over the old, unpainted drywall. We wasted so much  wall paint and time because the old walls just sucked it up. When we finally started with primer to seal the wall, the wall finish went on beautifully.

Great video
Unless you are experienced at doing tape and plaster, it is better left to a professional. you may be the 1 in 1000 person who is a natural... or maybe like 1 in 10000... but odds are definitely against that. The problem is, the quality of the plaster finish is what you are going to see. You can do as good a job as possible on everything else, but if the finish on the plaster isn't good, that is all you will see for year to come. And trust me, once you prime/paint it, you will definitely see your mistakes. I hate to be negative, but you also have left yourself a lot of butt joints from the looks of how you hung the drywall. These are no sweat for a professional, but they are much harder for a do-it-yourselfer. I am speaking from experience here... I do absolutely everything myself... everything, except plaster and tape. If you want to be pleased with the finished product, invest a small amount of money and have someone who knows what they are doing finish it. Just a hint, most guys who do this will also want to prime it, that is when they will see their mistakes and go back over to fix them. If you decide it isn't that important (the finished product) then good luck! Either way it looks a hell of a lot better than when you started.

TIPS from been there - done that - many times : NEW mud and tape. Proper tools. A "V" trowel is a good tool. Self adhesive tape is helpful if you have the budget. And necessary for small goofups and gaps / plugs Small and large spreader trowels on hand - and maybe a square "hawk". ( hand held platform - mud holder ) THIN COATS. Spread out over multiple days is easier than KILLING yourself - your lungs - your arms and filling your house with dust.  Thick coats will crack anyway and then require deep sanding and redoing it. Frustrating. Time consuming. Proper temp and humidity ( if possible) in the rooms - for the drying periods. You might consider plastic sheeting the room openings before sanding. Dust will go everywhere - and into other rooms. Have a wet dry vac on hand with good filter and working properly. Proper masks and even eye coverings for sanding stages. Close tubs tight if any left over - or use all remaining in tubs. CAREFUL if doing mudding / taping / sanding on ladders up high. ( Scaffolding ? ) or at very least an "A" frame type ladder - or ladders with wood beams to stand on ( homemade scaffold )  IF using days old mud - add water and mix thoroughly. And simply skimming off top dry portion works.  Rent a lift for ceiling panels if full sized. And lastly - get a few DRYWALL BITS with a concave holder mechanism. They are like $ 2 or less - and are incredibly helpful. And stop drill from going too deep.
Drywallschool.com and less is better! Underfill and more coats is better than days of sanding. Get covers for all the furniture and such before sanding bc the sand will literally go everywhere.

Having hung drywall, and taped, spackled, sanded, spackled, sanded, spackled sanded, spackled sanded.... did I say spackled then sanded? No? Repeat. Over time you will get the technique for taping and mud. Suggest not to use pre mixed joint compound, instead invest in mixable compound, adding a small amount of plaster of Paris will speed up the drying time on your mud job. Get graduated in size â€œKnivesâ€ (aka putty knives) from smallest in width to widest. professional mud applicators have a machine that tapes and muds simultaneously. Donâ€™t know if you can rent one. But if you do decide to tackle this yourself practice on a scap piece of drywall. Learning how to feather your application is the biggest hurdle. One piece of advice, once you smear out your compound, donâ€™t try to go back and resmear it. Forget using the mesh tape, thatâ€™s for repairing holes, always use paper tape. A tip - if you don't know this already - ROTOZIP - or equivalent generic off brand one. Makes cutouts so much simpler - easier - faster. And best technique is to use the bit as a "router" of sorts to ride along studs or framing. You punch through - got to stud or frame slowly / carefully - then ride the surface all along to get a perfect cut out of a window - door etc . .

Like a few others have said, mudding and taping arenâ€™t actually that bad if you thin out the mud, wipe it down with a very wet sponge (not damp, submerged and then quickly squeezed, still dripping water is what you want)  right away and just accept doing several very thin layers. Pros can do it well with thicker mud and fewer layers, but thatâ€™s only because of practice. Youâ€™ll be sanding to get things nice and smooth between layers, but sanding can only do so much. The point where people really start cursing the job and swearing to hire pros in the future is when they realize they made things too thick and uneven for the sanding to do much good. Thus the thin layers and sponging down to really smooth it before it dries.   Also, the degree to which the dust will get everywhere cannot be exaggerated. Use twice as much plastic to protect the kitchen, etc. as you think youâ€™ll need.  Iâ€™d hang it from he loft to the floor between the living room and dining room but also drape your dining table and kitchen counters in it while youâ€™re working. Especially since you are going to be eating in that space during the time youâ€™re doing the job, you want to make extra sure you donâ€™t need to wash every dish before every lunch and dinner can even be prepared. Laying plastic everywhere will feel like overkill but it truly isnâ€™t.

YES - drywall is INCREDIBLY taxing physically - very labor intensive - takes a long time with multiple steps involved and even quite messy when you get to all the mudding - taping and sanding. And THEN you have to paint it. And even getting it to your place is a nightmare - with paid delivery being the best route. I HATE drywalling. And with 2 small people like yourselves - you have to be careful to not hurt yourselves - especially if you are attempting ceiling drywall. ( rent a lift ) Anyway - good luck. I drywalled my living room once, my living room was covered in plastic for 3 weeks from having to sand the mud over and over and over again. I drywall for a living. when you tape and mud you should 100% make sure to lay down paper so you dont get mud everywhere! I recommend Black Top All purpose Joint Compound for the first coat and Plus 3 Blue top mud for the second and third coats. Good luck!
you can do it, watch a few videos for the basics. I notice a few comments to sand between coats and generally that is not necessarily the best advice. rather use your putty knife to knock quickly knockoff the high points when the first coat is dry and lay down the next coat. Likely you will need three or four coats and sanding between coats will remove material that will just need to be be added back. the first and second coats will look rough but don't let that discourage you.

In the UK, mud and tape is called plastering. Anyone can do it, the difference being, when you run your finger over a professional's work it will feel like glass with almost no imperfections (lumps, scratches and divots); an amateur's work will look and feel like a cave wall. It is actually most difficult in warm weather, because it dries before you get a chance to get the smooth even finish. Even an experienced amatuer can expect a lot (hours) of sanding and filling once finished. You'll be fine!

Looking great!... Use acoustical sealant instead of  tuck tape for the sealing of your poly vapor barrier.  Dry wall tips hmm, well its pretty easy and the two of you will make it look so :) ... have it nice and warm inside...do a test patch and tape a long joint... use enough mud but little as possible... tools -> mud pan, 3 sizes of trowel knifes, corner trowel (just a cheap one... u only use for taping), drill if you need to mix, mixing paddle, Wallsand Drywall Sandpaper 3-5/16 Inch X11 Inch 150 Grit Handy-Pack (do not use the drywall screen) and a sanding pad........   mud mix to remove air bubbles you could do this in your mud pan by hand for the amount you have->tape->add a skim of mud over top-> trowel (smallest taping knife)and remove access-> let dry-> add another coat (next size bigger taping knife)-> let dry-> last coat use (widest taping knife)->let dry->sand->paint with primer or paint and primer-> fill in any defects->sand-> paint and prime spots->paint last time.... Just remember the more mud you have on the wall the more you have to sand... for any nail holes use the back of your trowel knife and dimple them in and fill.   Looking forward to seeing the resultsğŸ˜Š

The way I understand it is very hard to get a smooth finish on the mud and tape even the professionals have a hard time with it.
Get a proper mudding set-up.  13" spreader with a trough.  Use a 6" spreader for the 1st layer/ embedding the seam tape.   Use corner bead for outside corners.   Once dry, a light scrape with the 6" spreader to remove any loose or ptiud bits, then a coat with the big spreader to make a smooth transition across all the seams. Let it dry. A light sanding should do it.  I recommend that you get the sanding attachment for your shop vac.  Get the one witha pole.

I saw a few other comments but thought I'd offer my opinion as a contractor who primarily works on remodeling projects like you have here. Adding insulation in the walls of the guest bedroom would have been good for heat retention and sound barrier, but the opposite side in the kitchen/master bed may still be exposed so you still can put that up. Likewise in the ceiling if you can get to it from the loft.   In terms of mudding (or we call it taping in the States), I have a few recommendations: get a variety of knives (not actual knives, they're more like a flat scraper but we call the knives in my area) in different sizes for applying the compound. I'd recommend a 2", 4" and 8". Having an appropriate sized surface to spread will make a huge difference. Second, put at least three coats of compound on spread out among ~3 days (one coat per day). This part is tedious, but making sure each coat drys before the next goes on is essential. Related, be sure to sand only when the compound is fully dry, and do so between each coat. Also, keep in mind that this next part will make a huge mess as compound easily falls on the ground. Watch your step and clean up as you go to avoid spreading it through your entire home, which becomes a nightmare to clean up (it does NOT clean out of surfaces easily). Best of luck to you!

I would check out Ottawa Builder. He does a lot of DIY videos and has a whole drywall series that's brilliant and saved me a ton of time and headache! He gives you good tips on spreading tips, drying time, taping methods, shortcuts, etc. I would also look at Mr. Build It drywall tips. He talks about the perfect consistency to mix the mud to to make it easier to spread. Like ketchup or sour cream. And there are different grades of mud you can buy to mix. I have done drywall for over 30 years. You need to stagger your sheets and if possible install full sheets on the walls. Less chance for multiple cracks later on down the road. You need to get an adapter for your screw gun for the screws. If put into deep, you will have pop screws after awhile. When taping, use paper tape only. No mesh tape. Use all purpose compound for coating, especially the base coat. Do not use the gimmick easy sand compound. Put 3 coats on the joints. The last coat, mix down the compound to a consistency of putty.  This will help even out all your mistakes . Ask a question anytime. Good luck.

Guys fit the dry wall verticale, not horizontal. As there is a feather edge on both long sides of the dry wall sheet it makes it much easier to mud and feather when these too feathered edges are adjoining each other.  The top and bottom edges, which have no feather, are then covered by skirting and molding at the top ;). Mudding is easy, just takes a bit of practice, go for it :)

edit in: i havent deleted my initial comment but i have put tips from 20yrs of work in the field in the comment  i wish theyd contacted me, not that they would/could, iv had 2decades of residential/commercial drywall exp including mud work ... its _easy_ ... of all the aspects of construction drywall is the least stressful the mud can be applied with no more than 3 of the blades used, a damp sponge the day after to let the mud dry overnight and no need for sanding to make smooth easy easy work and if you can read a tape and know the stud centering its stupid simple ... in a 30min convo i can explain to all but the most dense how to get perfect joints with 5min practice on scrap material edit in, the time wasted making the 2nd cuts on the drywall is very rookie and hilarious, score break and snap off  mudding tips. thin out the mud a tiny bit. do it per tray not per box. i.e. when you load the mud tray add a little bit of water, mix it in lather rinse repeat until its creamy but not much thinner than when you dug it out of the mud box. youll know you got it right when you load a blade and the mud stays smooth and piled up, if its too dry itll break and crumble along the sides if its too thin it wont form a smooth pile on the blade itll flow off. wear clothes you do not mind never getting clean again put down a canvas drop cloth, plastic will fail do not trust it. wear shoes that can be taken off easily so when you leave the area covering by the drop cloth you can leave the shoes there and avoid tracking mud you will drop all over the house wear a bandana that completely covers your hair. trying to get dry mud you didnt realize was in your hair out is NOT FUN use regular yellow joint tape for inside corners, staple it up as high as you can and mud almost up to it before pulling the staple and finishing it. do not leave it to dry and come back the next day to deal with it, doing that will leave a lump you will have to remove before finishing. a 2 inch spatula blade is for working very tight areas and filling in small divots, you can get by with a 6inch and 12 inch blade but if you want to add a bit of a step you can use a 4inch blade to load a seam joint before putting the tape up, the 6inch blade will work fine for dragging the excess mud off and setting the tape. let the 1st application dry completely before doing the second pass and float pass you will get mud all over you, you will wonder how you got mud in your hair when you were only working on the walls and lower joints butt joints, areas where cut ends meet up will require extra work to smooth out. expect to float 16 inches out from butt joints to fade them in outside corners are best done with metal corner strips that come pretaped they will give you beautiful corners and hide those wavy cuts unless you put the cut to an inside corner and the seam joint to the outside, in which case still use the metal corner fitting it is always best to use a rigid corner material on an outside corner because the moisture in the joint compound aka mud will make getting a clean corner on the 'will be damp for days' cut drywall corner is _very_ time consuming and pros usually just rip out the dry wall and redo it seam corner out if theyre allowed because of how much effort is required to make a damp wavy edge into a nice clean corner regulations for float in commercial here in the states varies but is usually 24inches which makes it look like you are essentially plastering the wall and it isnt necessary. a good float can be done at 12 inches let the float coat dry completely then use a damp sponge to wipe off any ridges and make the float smooth as glass when taping start from a corner and work all the way across, working from the corners to the center will leave bumps and working from the center to the corners will waste lots of material trying to get it right before picking up a single mud tool or scrap of tape make absolutely sure all screws or nail heads are counter sunk, for screws the 2inch blade is ideal for mudding in the divots nails tend to require a 3 or 4 inch blade because to set then below flush you have to leave a hammer head divot which is often nearly an inch across and a 2inch blade will leave an odd depression/require more effort than necessary for seam joints on the walls the sticky mesh tape is perfect, you can put it directly in place, no mud required to hold it in place though a staple on 1 end will keep you from pulling it loose as you work just remember to remove the staple. i know this tip sounds like im repeating it a lot but unless you are fine with frustration every few minutes take it to heart but dont forget to remove them. MOST IMPORTANT MUD TIP do everything you can to avoid dry mud chips from the bag the mud came in from dropping into the mud your using, even a small chip will leave gouges and tracks that will frustrate you to no end. the best way toi avoid the problem for a short term at least is to move the mud from the bag its sealed in in the box to a bucket with a good lid. to do so do not untie the bag, lift it out entirely and while the strong person holds it over the bucket the person good at directing things rips a hole in the bottom and squeezes the mud into the bucket. when removing mud from the bucket avoid smearing mud up the inside of the bucket, it will dry and crumble and make everything else much much more frustrating after every full tray has been mostly used, i say mostly because by the time you get to the last dregs the mud has dried out considerably and there will be chunks of assorted detritus mixed in, wash out the tray, clean the blades, do not half ass it orr you will be further frustrated. if you take the time to watch some vids on the tapping and floating part and get an idea of how to do the main work you only need to remember that as long as you dont leave any major gouges or ridges you will not have to sand at all, a damp sponge will let you get rid of small ridges and smooth out the joint to a near polished state ... as long as you dont over do it corners are the mudmans bane if said mudder is inexperienced. inside corners are hardest because you are working 2 sides towards the center. use some scrap drywall to experiment with butt joints, seam joints and inside corners until you have an idea of what to do. its a time killer but for beginners that practice can mean the difference between a nice finish and a bunch of 'why did you punch the wall now we have to patch _that_ too!?'
